## Domain Knowledge
**Domain**: chemical reaction kinetics (rate laws / kinetic ODE modeling)
**Analysis**: The target dA_dt is explicitly the time-derivative of a concentration A, which is the standard form of kinetic rate laws written as an ODE. With only (t, A) available, the likely models are autonomous or time-varying rate laws (e.g., nth-order decay, reversible relaxation to equilibrium, or externally forced/time-dependent rate constants).

**Common formula structures** (built-in generic + LLM domain-specific):
- Arrhenius equation: k = c0 * exp(c1 / T) (chemical reaction rate dependence on temperature)
- Coulomb's / Newton's inverse-square law: F = c0 / r**2 (gravitational or electrostatic force)
- Stefan-Boltzmann law: P = c0 * T**4 (blackbody radiation power vs temperature)
- Beer-Lambert law: I = c0 * exp(c1 * x) (light intensity attenuation through a medium)
- Michaelis-Menten kinetics: v = c0 * S / (S + c1) (enzyme-catalyzed reaction saturation)
- Hill equation: y = c0 * x**c1 / (x**c1 + c2) (cooperative binding, sigmoidal dose-response)
- Kepler's third law: T**2 = c0 * a**3 (orbital period vs semi-major axis)
- Newton's law of cooling: T = c0 + c1 * exp(c2 * t) (exponential thermal relaxation)
- Clausius-Clapeyron relation: log(P) = c0 + c1 / T (vapor pressure vs temperature)
- Langmuir adsorption isotherm: theta = c0 * P / (1 + c1 * P) (surface adsorption equilibrium)
- Freundlich isotherm: q = c0 * C**c1 (empirical adsorption on heterogeneous surface)
- Stokes' drag: F = c0 * r * v (viscous drag force on a sphere in fluid)
- Logistic growth: N = c0 / (1 + c1 * exp(c2 * t)) (population dynamics with carrying capacity)
- Planck-Wien approximation: I = c0 * f**3 * exp(c1 * f / T) (thermal radiation spectrum)
- Zero-order consumption: dA_dt = -c0 (constant-rate depletion; surface-limited or excess reactant conditions)
- Zero-order with source term: dA_dt = c0 - c1 (net constant production/consumption; chemostat-like input/output simplified)
- First-order decay: dA_dt = -c0 * A (unimolecular decay, radioactive/thermal decomposition, pseudo-first-order kinetics)
- First-order with baseline production: dA_dt = c0 - c1 * A (linear relaxation with source; approach to steady state)
- Second-order decay: dA_dt = -c0 * A**2 (bimolecular reaction with equal reactants or self-reaction)
- General nth-order rate law: dA_dt = -c0 * A**c1 (empirical reaction order; mass-action-like generalization)
- Autocatalytic / growth-with-limitation rate form: dA_dt = c0 * A * (c1 - A) (autocatalysis with finite resource/precursor; fixed points at A=0 and A=c1)
- Reversible relaxation to equilibrium (linear): dA_dt = -c0 * (A - c1) (first-order approach to equilibrium concentration c1)
- Reversible nonlinear relaxation (mass-action-inspired): dA_dt = -c0 * A**c1 + c2 (forward consumption balanced by constant back-production approximation)
- Inhibition / saturation in the rate: dA_dt = -c0 * A / (A + c1) (self-inhibition, limited catalyst/active sites, transport limitation proxy)
- Time-varying first-order rate (activation with time): dA_dt = -c0 * exp(c1 * t) * A (externally ramped conditions increasing effective rate with time)
- Time-varying first-order rate (deactivation/decay of activity): dA_dt = -c0 * exp(-c1 * t) * A (catalyst deactivation or diminishing driving force)
- Periodic forcing of rate: dA_dt = -c0 * (1 + c1 * sin(c2 * t)) * A (periodic light/temperature modulation affecting kinetics)
- Mixed-order / polynomial kinetics: dA_dt = -c0 * A - c1 * A**2 (parallel unimolecular + bimolecular consumption pathways)
- Time-weakening apparent rate (empirical): dA_dt = -c0 * A / (t + c1) (aging/conditioning effects where apparent rate decreases with elapsed time)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Consumption vs production: predominantly negative dA_dt for A>0 suggests depletion; a positive offset term (e.g., +c0) indicates feed/generation maintaining A.
- Reaction order signature: linear dependence on A indicates first-order; quadratic (A**2) indicates bimolecular/self-reaction; fractional power indicates complex/empirical order.
- Equilibrium behavior: a factor (A - c1) or a polynomial with real roots in A implies fixed points (steady concentrations) and relaxation toward/away from equilibrium.
- Parallel pathways: sums of different powers of A (e.g., -c0*A - c1*A**2) indicate multiple simultaneous mechanisms contributing to net rate.
- Time-dependent environment: multiplicative functions of t (exp(c*t), sin(c*t), 1/(t+c)) represent changing temperature/light/catalyst activity over the course of the experiment.
- Inhibition/transport limitation: rational forms like A/(A+c1) produce a rate that becomes less sensitive to increasing A, consistent with saturation of active sites or diffusion limits.
- Autocatalysis indicators: rate terms proportional to A times another decreasing factor in A (e.g., A*(c1-A)) imply self-acceleration at low A and self-limitation at high A.
- Near-zero behavior: if dA_dt does not vanish at A=0 (e.g., dA_dt=c0), the system can create A from a precursor pool; if dA_dt=0 at A=0, A=0 is an absorbing state.
- Sign changes in dA_dt over A: indicates regimes where A increases below a threshold and decreases above it, consistent with stable steady states or feedback-controlled kinetics.

**GT:** `-0.5475*A² - 0.5475*A*exp(-0.03708*t) + 0.7049*cos(log(A + 1))`

The GT has three distinct basis functions:
1. `A²` (polynomial)
2. `A*exp(-0.03708*t)` (exponential decay times A)
3. `cos(log(A + 1))` (cosine of logarithm)

The key challenge is that `cos(log(A+1))` is a very specific transcendental function. Let me check which candidates could possibly reproduce this.

**Critical observation:** The term `cos(log(A+1))` cannot be represented by:
- Polynomials in A
- `A^(1/3)` or `(A+c)^(1/3)` terms
- `A/(A+c)` rational terms
- `log(A+c)` terms
- `exp(A*c)` terms
- `sin(A*c)` terms (different from cos(log(A+1)))

None of the candidates contain `cos(log(...))`. Let me verify this by checking each one:

- Candidates 1-55: I see terms like `(A+c)^(1/3)`, `A/(A+c)`, `exp(c*t)`, `log(A*c+1)`, `sin(A*c+c)`, `exp(A*c)`, `|A|^c`, `log(c*exp(...)+1)`, `t^c`, etc.

**None** of the candidates contain `cos(log(A+c))` or any equivalent form.

The function `cos(log(A+1))` is linearly independent from all the basis functions present in any candidate. It cannot be exactly represented by polynomials, rational functions, cube roots, exponentials, simple logarithms, or simple trigonometric functions of A.

Some candidates might try to approximate `cos(log(A+1))` using other functions, but per Rule 2, approximations are forbidden - we need EXACT equivalence.

Let me verify: Could `cos(log(A+1))` be zero? No, at A=0, cos(log(1)) = cos(0) = 1, and it's multiplied by 0.7049, so it contributes a non-zero constant and varies with A.

Could the cos(log(A+1)) term be absorbed? At A=0, it gives 0.7049. As A grows, it oscillates (very slowly). No combination of the basis functions in any candidate can reproduce this oscillatory behavior in log-space.

Therefore, **no candidate matches**.
